Identified_Attack:
- Most Likely: Attack 3
- Confidence: High
- Reasoning:
  The query day is a Sunday (same weekday as reference Day 0). Reference Sunday consumption shows a clear diurnal pattern with minimum 21.06, maximum 63.82, mean 40.58. The query day has min 0.00, max 25.72, mean 9.85 – a drastic reduction (~76% mean drop) and many near‑zero readings. The shape is distorted but still retains a faint diurnal outline (morning dip, evening peak), indicating the original profile is partially preserved yet severely altered per sample. This matches Attack 3 (per‑sample random scaling) where each original value is multiplied by an independent random factor in (0,1). Many samples become extremely small (e.g., 0.00‑0.84), while others retain moderate magnitude (up to 0.4× original peak). 
  Top alternatives ruled out:
  - Attack 2 (on‑off attack): would produce contiguous blocks of exact zeros. Here zeros are sparse, not in long blocks, and many “zero” intervals actually show tiny positive values (0.20, 0.84), not exact 0.00.
  - Attack IEEE: alternates between reporting real consumption and zero. The non‑zero intervals would be close to the reference day’s values, but here the highest reading is only 25.72 vs. the original peak of 63.82, and pseudo‑zero intervals are not exact zeros. Scaling, not switching, is the dominant effect.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- Each 15‑minute consumption sample of the day is multiplied by a different random factor between 0 and 1. This factor varies independently for every reading, causing some intervals to be heavily suppressed (near zero) while others are only partially reduced.
- Impact: the overall magnitude is sharply reduced (mean drops from ~40.6 to ~9.9). The original trend is broken but still faintly recognizable; the attack introduces high‑frequency variability that distorts both trend and amplitude.
- Possible technical causes: meter bypass with intermittent magnetic interference, reverse current injection causing random scaling of reported values, or false data injection where the attacker applies per‑sample multiplicative tampering.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Attack 0,1,4,6,7,9,10,12 are ruled out.

This block coincides with the Monday evening peak visible in the reference week, but the magnitude is drastically reduced and the values are irregular (including an internal zero at index 82). This pattern matches Attack 3 (random per‑sample multiplicative factor) because the zeros can correspond to factors of 0, while the non‑zero values are scaled‑down versions of the original high evening consumption. Attack 2 (on‑off zeroing) was considered but ruled out because the non‑zero window still reports severely reduced consumption (max 5.71 vs. expected ~20); Attack 2 replaces only with zeros and leaves actual readings unchanged. Attack 7 (subtract constant cutoff) was considered but no single cutoff could simultaneously produce the observed array—especially the zero at index 82 where original consumption was over 20—while matching the other non‑zero values.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated: Each 15‑minute interval’s actual consumption is multiplied by a random factor between 0 and 1 (unique per sample). For the vast majority of intervals, the factor is zero (or effectively zero), yielding reported zeros. During a short period in the late evening, the random factors take non‑zero values, producing a low, noisy, scaled‑down impression of the true evening peak.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The overall magnitude collapses (daily mean from 9–10 to 0.32). The normal weekly trend of two daily peaks is obliterated; only a faint, irregular spike remains in the evening. Both trend and amplitude are fundamentally altered.
- Possible technical causes: This behavior can result from intermittent magnetic interference causing random attenuation of the current measurement, a partial meter bypass with sporadic reconnection, or malicious false data injection where scaled‑down random values are reported instead of the true consumption.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Attack 9: constant average. Not.

Attack 10: reverse pattern. Not.

Alternatively, maybe it's Attack 2 combined with a meter that is also under-reporting, but we stick to defined attacks.

Let's also consider Attack IEEE might produce some zeros and some actual, but here the "actual" are not actual. So no.

Therefore, I'll classify as Manipulated, most likely Attack 3. Alternatives considered: Attack 2 and Attack 7, explaining why they were ruled out.

- Most Likely: Attack 3
- Confidence: Medium
- Reasoning: The query day shows an overwhelming number of zero readings with a short segment of low, varying non-zero values around the evening peak. The evening segment closely tracks the time-of-day pattern of the reference Monday's evening peak but with significantly reduced magnitude and irregular fluctuations (including a zero within the segment). This matches Attack 3 (per-sample random scaling factor) because the zeros can result from factors of 0, while the low non-zero values correspond to factors between 0 and 1 applied to the original high evening consumption. Attack 2 (zero-for-random-duration) was considered but ruled out because the non-zero window still reports severely reduced consumption (max 5.71 vs. expected ~20), which is inconsistent with the definition that only zeros are substituted, not scaled. Attack 7 (subtract cutoff) was considered but could not produce the observed pattern with any single constant cutoff, as the non-zero values do not fit a common shift; the sequence includes a zero amidst positive values (index 82) where the original consumption was very high, which would remain positive under any reasonable cutoff.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated: Each 15-minute interval's actual consumption is multiplied by a random factor between 0 and 1 (unique per sample). For most intervals, the factor is 0 or extremely close to 0, resulting in reported zeros. For a block of intervals in the late evening (roughly 6 PM – 9 PM), the random factors take non-zero values, producing a scaled-down and irregular version of the true evening peak.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The overall magnitude is drastically reduced (daily mean from ~9–10 down to 0.32). The original weekly trend (morning and evening peaks) is completely lost, replaced by a single short burst of low, noisy consumption. The trend is altered because strong zeroing during the daytime removes the normal daytime usage pattern, leaving only a distorted remnant of the evening peak.
- Possible technical causes: This behavior is typically caused by intermittent manipulation at the meter level, such as a magnetic interference that randomly attenuates the current measurement, a meter bypass with irregular reconnection, or false data injection where the attacker injects randomly scaled values instead of true readings.

- Confidence: High
- Reasoning: The query day contains long stretches of zero readings and non-zero values that are not original (max 73.13 vs normal min 71.73). When non-zero, the values are proportional to the original readings at corresponding times but with highly variable scaling factors (e.g., 0.1, 0.3, 0.6, 0.7), indicating per-sample multiplication. Attack 2 and Attack IEEE were ruled out because they would retain original values during “on” or non-zero intervals, not scaled-down values. Attack 7 (constant subtraction) fails because no single offset produces the observed values, and Attack 6 (capping) would create a plateau at the cutoff, not the diverse range seen.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:

Many factors are zero, zeroing out extended periods; others are moderate (0.1–0.7), producing a reduced, distorted version of the original pattern.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Overall magnitude is severely reduced (mean drops from ~109 to 22.6). The trend is altered because the zero blocks and varying factors break the typical daily rhythm, though some time-of-day structure remains faintly.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

So the day has long zero periods, some isolated small values, and a cluster of moderate values (max 73.13) that appear to be much lower than the normal peaks. The normal pattern for Day 2 in reference shows typical morning low (80-90s) then a midday peak (~130, then later afternoon peak ~160-189). The query day shows zeros overnight and into morning, then a short burst of readings peaking around 70, then zeros again, then another small cluster in the late evening reaching 73 max, then fading. This is not consistent with normal behavior. The attack seems to be a mix of zero periods and some reduced readings, but the zero periods are extensive. The query day has many zeros (most of the day) and when non-zero, they are still significantly reduced (max 73 vs normal min 71, normal mean 108). So the overall consumption is drastically lower.

Now consider attack types:

Attack 0: all zero. Not this, because there are non-zero readings.
Attack 1: multiply by constant factor between 0,1. If constant factor, then the shape would be identical but scaled. Here, the shape is entirely different: there are long zero stretches where normally there are non-zero values (e.g., early morning normally 80-90, here are 0; late night normally ~100, here are 0 or tiny). So Attack 1 would preserve the trend exactly, just lower magnitude. That doesn't match.
Attack 2: on-off attack: replace with zeros for random duration. This involves zeroing out some periods, but when on, the readings are normal. Here, when non-zero, the readings are still very low (max 73, which is less than the minimum normal consumption of 71.73? Actually normal min is 71.73, so 73 is just barely above that min. But many non-zero readings are 30-70, which is lower than the typical lows of 80+. So the "on" periods are not original values; they are reduced as well. So Attack 2 wouldn't match because in Attack 2, during "on" periods the consumption is original. Here, even the non-zero readings are reduced.
Attack 3: multiply by different random factors per sample. That would scale each sample independently. That could produce some zeros if the factor is 0 for some samples. But it would generally produce a noisy version of the original, not long contiguous zero blocks. The zeros here appear in long continuous stretches (first 15 intervals all zero, then a break, then zeros again). That pattern could be generated if many consecutive factors are zero, but that's less likely; Attack 3 typically multiplies each sample by a random factor from (0,1), but to get many consecutive zeros, the random factors would have to be exactly zero for many consecutive samples. That's possible but Attack 3 is described as "different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample". It doesn't specify distribution; it could include 0 with some probability. But in practice, random factors might rarely be exactly zero unless designed. The query day has long runs of zero. Attack 3 would produce a more irregular pattern, not such systematic zero blocks. So Attack 3 is less plausible.
Attack 4: randomly selected period within the observation window, all measurements in that period are reduced. That would mean one contiguous period is reduced, while the rest is normal. Here, the zeros appear in two separate blocks (beginning and middle, and end) with a reduced period in between. That's more than one period. So not Attack 4.
Attack 5: replace each sample by multiplying average normal consumption by random variable between 0 and 1. This would produce completely random values unrelated to the original profile. The values in the query day are not just random; they show some structure (zeros, then a spike, then a cluster somewhat resembling a daily pattern but compressed and reduced). Attack 5 would likely produce no correlation with time-of-day, just random noise. Here, the non-zero cluster occurs in the midday and evening, which might align with normal high-consumption times. So it's not completely random; there is some temporal pattern. So Attack 5 unlikely.
Attack 6: a random cut-off point selected, all values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. That would cap the values, so any original reading > cutoff becomes cutoff, but anything below remains. The query day has many zeros; that would only happen if the cutoff is 0, then any positive value becomes 0? Actually Attack 6: "A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value." So if cutoff is X, then any reading > X becomes X. Values ≤ X remain as is. This means there would be no values above X. In the query day, there are values up to 73.13. If the cutoff was say 70, then anything above 70 becomes 70, so there would be a cap at 70. But we see values of 73.13, 68.91, 67.51, etc. That's not a clear cap; max is 73.13, min non-zero is 1.4. The original normal day had values above 70 most of the time. If cutoff were 73, many values would become 73, but we see a variety below 73. Attack 6 would result in a flat plateau at the cutoff value for all originally high periods. That is not observed. So not Attack 6.
Attack 7: subtract a random cut-off point from each sample; if negative, zero. This would shift the whole profile down by a constant. Now, if Attack 7 subtracts a constant C, then query[i] = max(0, original[i] - C). To see if there is a C that works, compute differences for positive values. The factors vary widely (from 0.012 to 0.721), and many are around 0.3-0.6 for the main cluster. There are also many zeros (factor 0). This fits Attack 3: each sample multiplied by a unique random factor. The attack changes both trend and amplitude, which matches the complete distortion of the pattern. Attack 3 could be caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, false data injection. So Attack 3 is the most likely.

Alternatives to consider: Attack 5 would have completely random values, but here values correlate with original times (non-zero during typically active periods), so not purely random. Attack 2 would have original values when on, but here values are reduced, so no. Attack 4 one period, not multiple. Attack IEEE would have original values mixed with zeros, but here reduced. So Attack 3 is best.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: Each 15‑minute reading is multiplied by a unique random number between 0 and 1. Consequently, some intervals (e.g., the early morning) are reduced by 80–100%, while late‑afternoon/evening readings are reduced by only 10–20%. One reading became exactly 0.0 because its random factor was effectively zero.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The overall daily mean drops from ~141.3 to 94.7 (a 33% reduction), and the minimum falls to 0.0 from the normal 57. The shape of the daily trend is preserved in outline but becomes noisier, with different segments suppressed to varying degrees, altering both the amplitude and the fine‑grained trend.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Let's compare ratios at indices where reference is not too low. At index 48: query 177.55, ref 202.85 -> ratio 0.875. Index 49: 173.99 vs 217.08 -> 0.801. Index 50: 170.43 vs 217.08 -> 0.785. So the evening ratios are higher (0.8) compared to morning (0.2-0.5). That suggests a time-dependent attack. Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." That would create a period with lower values and a period with normal values. Here, the entire day seems reduced, but the reduction factor varies: morning extremely reduced, afternoon less so. That could be a period where all measurements are reduced (the whole day is reduced, but maybe the reduction factor is not constant? Attack 4 says "all measurements in that period are reduced" but does not specify if it's a constant reduction or multiplication. It could be multiplication by a factor? The description says "All measurements in that period are reduced." It doesn't specify how, but typically it means multiplied by a factor or subtracted. Attack 3 multiplies each sample by a different random factor, which would create varying ratios throughout the day. That could explain why the ratios are not constant across the day. However, Attack 3 would typically produce a noisier pattern because the factor varies per sample, so adjacent readings could have very different ratios. Our ratios vary somewhat smoothly? Let's check adjacent ratios: index 0 ratio 0.5, index 1 ratio 0.55, index 2 ratio 0.497, index 3 0.528, index 4 0.583. Not extremely noisy. Smoothness is not guaranteed with random factors either because they are random per sample, so they can be any value between 0 and 1. They could be similar by chance. So Attack 3 is a candidate.

Also consider Attack 7: subtracts a cut-off. If we subtract a fixed value from each sample, the ratio would not be constant, and the minimum would become zero if the cut-off is high enough. The query day has a minimum of 0.00, which could result from subtraction. Let's test if a fixed subtraction yields similar pattern. Suppose original reference Day 2 values minus some constant C equals query day values. For index 0: 128.11 - C = 63.67 => C = 64.44. Index 1: 128.11 - 64.44 = 63.67, but query index 1 is 70.78, not matching. So not a fixed subtractor. Maybe a different subtractor per sample? Attack 7 uses a random cut-off point subtracted, not a fixed cut-off for all samples. It says: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported." That could be interpreted as a single random cut-off point for the whole day, i.e., subtract the same constant from every sample. That's what I just tested and it didn't match. So Attack 7 is ruled out.

Attack 6: replaces values above a cut-off with the cut-off. That would cap values, not reduce low values. Our query day has very low values in the morning, so not a cap.

Attack 5: each sample replaced by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable. The average normal consumption of reference Day 2 is 141.27. Multiplying by random (0,1) would give values between 0 and 141.27. Our query day max is 191.78 > 141.27, so that doesn't fit because max exceeds average*1. So Attack 5 is out.

Attack 0: all zeros, not our case.

Attack 1: multiply by a single constant random factor. The ratios vary too much for a single constant, so rule out.

Attack 2: on-off attack, random duration of zeros. That would produce a block of consecutive zeros. We have only one isolated zero, not a block. So not Attack 2.

Attack 9: flat constant profile, no.

Attack 10: reversal of consumption pattern, no.

Attack 12: replacement with lower-consuming user's pattern. That could explain a different shape but the shape looks similar to the reference day shape (low morning, high afternoon/evening) but scaled. It might be a different user's pattern, but likely it's a manipulated version of the same user's pattern because the pattern is temporally aligned (morning low, peak at similar times). So attack 12 is possible but less likely without specific evidence.

Attack IEEE: substitution with zero for some intervals. That would produce zeros exactly where the attacker chooses to zero out. Here we have exactly one zero reading. Could be IEEE. But what about the other low values? If the attacker reports actual consumption for some intervals and zero for others, the non-zero values should match the reference (or be close, allowing for noise). However, the non-zero values in query day are significantly lower than reference, not matching. So they are not actual consumption. Therefore, it's not simply zeroing some and leaving others as actual. So IEEE is ruled out.

Thus the most likely attacks are those that involve scaling down the whole day with varying factors: Attack 3 (random per-sample factor) or Attack 4 (selected period reduced). Attack 4 would reduce a random period, leaving the rest unchanged. If the selected period covers the morning hours, then morning would be reduced and evening might be original or slightly reduced. In our case, evening values are also reduced (max 191.78 vs 217.08, ratio ~0.88). So perhaps the reduced period covers most of the day, but the reduction factor might be constant within that period? Attack 4: "All measurements in that period are reduced." It doesn't specify exact mechanism. If it's a multiplicative reduction by a constant factor during that period, then we would expect a block of constant factor. Looking at the ratios, they vary from 0.2 to 0.9, so not a single factor during that period. However, the definition of Attack 4 might be vague. From the description: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." It could involve using a fixed factor for that period, but maybe the reduction can be via multiplication by a factor in (0,1) that could be constant or varying? It's ambiguous. Attack 3 explicitly multiplies each sample by a different random factor, which fits the varying ratios exactly.

Given the data, the query day's pattern is similar to the reference Tuesday but scaled down with a varying factor that is more severe in the early morning, less so later. That's consistent with Attack 3, where each sample is multiplied by a random factor between 0 and 1. Since the factors are random per sample, they can produce any pattern, including the observed one. The zero value can occur if the factor for that sample was 0 (a random value between 0 and 1 could be exactly 0, but it's a continuous distribution, unlikely to hit exactly zero. However, if the factor can be 0 in the simulation, it's possible. Or maybe the actual consumption was very low and rounded to zero? But the reference at that time is 56.94, so a factor of 0 would produce 0. If the random factor is drawn from a distribution that includes 0 (e.g., uniform(0,1) with a non-zero probability of 0? Actually uniform(0,1) has zero probability of exactly 0, but in practice digital representation could yield 0.0. So it's possible but rare. Alternatively, Attack 4 could allow a zero if the reduction is to zero.

But let's examine the zero reading: it's at index 24, where reference Day 2 has 56.94. A factor of 0 would give 0. It's plausible. Also the reading before it is 10.29 (index 23, ref 56.94 => factor ~0.18), and after it is 6.73 (index 25, ref 56.94 => factor ~0.12). So there's a cluster of very low factors around that time. That doesn't rule out Attack 3.

Attack 4 could also be a candidate if the reduced period is from around index 0 to maybe index 50, and reduction factor varies? But Attack 4 description doesn't explicitly say varying factor; it just says "reduced". Usually, attack 4 in literature is a scaling attack over a random period with a constant factor. So I'd lean towards Attack 3 as the most specific match.

Given the need to output confidence and reasoning, I'll choose Attack 3 as the most likely because the factors are not constant and not block-constant. The zero value could be a random factor of exactly 0 or due to rounding. The varying factors across the day match Attack 3's "unique factor per sample".

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

1. 3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

If the observation window is the whole day, and the random period is the entire day except a short evening window? That would mean the reduction period is almost the whole day, and the non-reduced period is the evening. But the evening is still reduced. Attack 4 could be that the theft occurs over a randomly selected period, and during that period, all measurements are reduced. The rest are normal. In the query day, the evening period has some non-zero values, but they are not normal; they are reduced. So if the theft period is the evening, then those are reduced, but what about the rest? The rest are zero. That could happen if the theft period covers the whole day? Then everything is reduced, but why are some zero? If reduction factor is severe, many become zero? For example, Attack 4 might multiply by a factor or subtract. The definition doesn't specify how they are reduced. It just says "All measurements in that period are reduced." It could be similar to Attack 1 or 3. So Attack 4 is vague. Attack 3 is per-sample scaling factors, which could produce zeros and small values. So maybe Attack 3 is the best fit because it says "the actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample." If many factors are zero or very low, we get zeros and low values. The contiguous block of zeros could be due to many consecutive zero factors, which is unlikely but possible if the attacker intentionally sets zero for a block and then random low factors later. Attack 3 doesn't specify if the factors are independent and identically distributed; they could be structured. So I think Attack 3 is plausible if the attacker uses zero for many intervals and small random for the rest. However, another attack: Attack IEEE is described as "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero." That is exactly on-off per interval, but the actual consumption would be reported in the "on" intervals. In the query day, the "on" intervals are not actual; they are low. So Attack IEEE is not matching.

Given the data, I suspect this is Attack 2 (on-off) with additional scaling during on periods, but the strict definition of Attack 2 doesn't include scaling. But maybe in practice, when the meter is bypassed partially, it might report zero or low values. However, the provided definitions are fixed, so we must choose.

Let's re-read Attack 2: "Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. ... Changes both the trend and amplitude." It does not say the rest are unmodified. It says "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration." This implies that during that random duration, they are zero; the other samples might be whatever the original is. So if the other samples are also modified, it's not Attack 2. In the query day, the non-zero samples are not original; they are very low. So Attack 2 doesn't fit.

Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." Here, the entire day is the observation window, and a random period (maybe the evening?) the measurements are reduced. But most of the day is zero, not reduced. If the random period covers the whole day, then all measurements are reduced. But then the reduction mechanism might make some zero and some low. So Attack 4 is possible if the reduction is such that many become zero. But the definition says "All measurements in that period are reduced" - reduced doesn't necessarily mean zero, but it could be reduced to zero. So if the theft period is the whole day, and the reduction is to multiply by a factor close to zero, we'd get zeros and low values. However, that would be Attack 1 or 3. Attack 4 is ambiguous. Maybe the intended meaning in NTL datasets is that Attack 4 selects a contiguous subperiod and within that subperiod, the values are multiplied by a factor (like Attack 1) or set to zero? I'm not sure.

Attack 3 is more specific: "multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample." So the factor could be sampled from a distribution that can be zero? But "between 0 and 1" might be inclusive of 0? Typically, if it's between 0 and 1, it could be exactly 0. But they'd say "between (0,1)" in parentheses, which might exclude 0. The description says "a constant random value between (0,1)" for Attack 1, and Attack 3 says "different random factors between 0 and 1" (without parentheses). It's ambiguous. However, if a factor is 0, the sample becomes zero. So Attack 3 can produce zeros. And the query day has many zeros, so that fits. But the contiguous block suggests the factors for those intervals were deliberately set to zero, not just random. Attack 3 doesn't specify that factors are independent; maybe the attacker can choose a block of zeros. So I'll consider Attack 3 as likely because it includes per-sample variation and can produce zeros.

Another candidate: Attack 2 might be implemented as on-off with the "on" periods being actual, but maybe the actual consumption in June is much lower? Unlikely, because summer (June) might have higher consumption due to cooling? Actually, summer could have higher. So the reference week in May had means around 30. The query day in June has mean 0.90, so clearly not seasonal variation.

Attack 0 is all zeros, but we have non-zeros.

Attack 6 (cap) and Attack 7 (subtract cutoff) we already ruled out.

Attack 12: pattern from lower-consuming user would have a different shape, not all zeros.

Attack 10: reverses pattern, not.

Attack 5: random around average, would not have many zeros.

Attack 9: constant, no.

So the likely ones are Attack 2 (on-off with zeros only) if we assume the non-zero values are actual but from a different pattern, but they are too low. Or Attack 3 if we allow zero factors. Or Attack 4 if we consider the whole day as theft period with severe reduction. But given the query day's statistic: min=0, max=11.17, mean=0.90, std=2.23. That's a very sparse consumption. In many NTL benchmark datasets, this pattern is typical of "Attack 2" where the attacker switches the meter off for most of the day and on for a short period. However, in the standard NTL attacks used in papers (like the one from IEEE Dataport), Attack 2 is precisely defined as replacing with zeros for a random duration, and the rest is unchanged. The unchanged rest would have normal values. Since the query day's non-zero values are not normal, it might be a different attack. Perhaps the attack is Attack 4 where the reduction period is the whole day, and the reduction function is a scaling factor that varies per sample (like Attack 3). But then it's a combination. The problem asks to identify the single most probable attack from the list. I'll go with Attack 3 because it involves varying factors per sample, which can result in zeros and low values, and it changes trend and amplitude. The pattern of zeros and the small values in the evening could be due to multiplicative factors: many factors near zero for the first part, and then factors slightly higher for the evening part. This could happen if the attacker uses different scaling at different times, mimicking a varying bypass.

Alternatively, Attack IEEE is "for each 15-minute interval, the consumer either reports actual or zero". That would give a mix of actual and zero. Actual values would be like the reference day's values. Since the non-zero values are not actual, it's not that.

I'll lean towards Attack 3. But wait, there is also Attack 2 with the possibility that the meter is only partially bypassed during the "on" times? Not in definition. So I'll choose Attack 3.

Now for confidence: Medium. Because the contiguous zero block is suspicious for Attack 3, but Attack 3 can produce it if factors are zero for a block.

Reasoning: compare to Attack 2 (on-off): ruled out because the non-zero samples are not at normal consumption levels. Attack 4: could be but Attack 3 more precisely describes per-sample varying reduction. Attack IEEE: ruled out because non-zero samples are not actual. Attack 0: all zeros. Attack 1: constant factor would not produce zeros. etc.

So my final: Classification: Manipulated. Most Likely: Attack 3. Confidence: Medium. Explanation: The day is manipulated; the consumption shows a long period of zeros followed by a period of low non-zero values. Attack 3 multiplies each sample by a random factor between 0 and 1, which can yield zeros when the factor is zero or near zero, and low values otherwise. This matches the pattern. Attack 2 was ruled out because the non-zero values are far below normal reference consumption. Attack IEEE was ruled out because non-zero values do not match actual consumption.

Attack behavior: The actual consumption values are multiplied by different random factors per sample, some factors being zero or very small, causing many zero readings and significantly reduced magnitude. The trend is altered because the normal daily cycle is replaced by zeros for most of the day and small values in the evening. Technical causes: false data injection, meter bypass with intermittent connection, or magnetic interference causing varying reduction.

- Reasoning: The query day shows a pattern of zeros mixed with low non‑zero values. This is precisely the signature of per‑sample random multiplicative factors (Attack 3): some factors are effectively zero, producing exact zeros, while others scale the original readings down by varying amounts, preserving the overall trend but obliterating the magnitude. Attack 2 (on‑off) was ruled out because the “on” periods would show full original consumption, not scaled values. Attack IEEE was ruled out because its definition requires either actual (full) consumption or zero, never a scaled intermediate.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: Each 15‑minute sample of the original consumption is multiplied by a different random factor between 0 and 1. When the factor is zero (or extremely close to zero), the reported value becomes zero; otherwise, the reading is a fraction of the true consumption. In the query day, long stretches of zeros correspond to periods where factors were zero or negligible, while the clustered low values (peaking around 20‑24) represent intervals where factors were somewhat larger, roughly 0.1‑0.3.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The overall magnitude collapses – the daily mean drops from ~72 to 4.17, and the maximum from ~104 to 24.04. The trend (time‑of‑day pattern) is partially retained: the morning and evening consumption humps are still discernible, but they are heavily attenuated and fragmented by the random zeroing and scaling.
- Possible technical causes: This attack can result from magnetic interference, reverse current, or false data injection that randomly alters the meter’s reported readings on a per‑sample basis, leading to an irregular but still shape‑related pattern of very low consumption.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Attack 1: multiplies by constant factor between 0 and 1. If factor were constant, the shape would be similar but scaled. That is structured, not random. So not Attack 5.

Attack 6: cut-off point; values above cut-off replaced by cut-off. If we pick a cut-off around 24, then all original values >24 would be replaced by 24. The day has some zero readings and some low readings. So not consistent.

What about Attack 2: "Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack." Could it be that the "random duration" is the periods outside the non-zero block? That would mean the "on" periods are the non-zero block, but the values in the on periods should be the original consumption. They are very low. So not.

Perhaps the attack is a combination of on-off and scaling? But we must pick the single most probable attack from the given list.

Let's consider the possibility that the day is original but with a very unusual consumption pattern. The mean is 4.17, min 0, max 24.04. In the reference week, the customer is a relatively high consumer (means 60-70). A day with mean 4.17 is extremely abnormal, almost no consumption. Could it be a day when the customer was away? This means if the interval is "on", the reported value is the true consumption. So we'd see a pattern of full values and zeros. But here the non-zero values are low. Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Look for any period with values similar to reference week. The evening peak 34.66 is lower than typical evening peak ~40, so probably no unreduced period.

Could this be Attack 2 (on-off)? "Replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration." No zeros here. So not that.

Attack 5: each sample replaced by average normal consumption * random(0,1). That would produce values centered around average*0.5 ~12, with no clear trend. The trend here is clear, so not that.

Attack 6: cut-off cap. Check if many values cluster around a particular threshold. The max is 34.66, but there are other values close to it: 33.86, 30.68, 29.09, 30.68, 34.66. Not a sharp cap. The distribution seems smooth.

Attack 7: subtract constant, zero floor. No zeros, but you could subtract a constant like 10, then values below 10 become 0, but our min is 4.4. So maybe subtract 5? Then 4.4 would be from original 9.4, but reference day min is 11.94, so not possible. So not that.

Attack 9: flat constant - not.

Attack 10: reverse pattern - the pattern isn't reversed (morning peak is still morning peak, it's not an evening peak). So no.

Attack 12: replaced with lower user pattern - the pattern shape is similar to this customer's normal shape (two peaks, timing), so unlikely.

So Attack 3 or Attack 1? But Attack 1 we ruled out based on min ratio. However, consider that the query day is a completely different day, and the "original" for that day is unknown. The customer's consumption may naturally vary. But the task says: "Given the two plots, determine whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation." The drastic drop in mean and min may indicate manipulation. But could it be a normal variation? The reference week shows relatively consistent daily patterns with means around 19-24, min around 10-12, max 37-47. The query day has min=4.4, mean=14.94, max=34.66. This is a significant departure. The standard deviation is similar, indicating the shape is preserved but shifted down. The most straightforward attack that reduces overall consumption while preserving trend is Attack 1 (constant scaling) or Attack 3 (varying scaling). But constant scaling would scale min proportionally. However, if the original day had min around 7 or 8, constant scaling could produce 4.4 with factor 0.6. But we never saw such low min in reference week. That suggests varying scaling, which can occasionally produce very low values.

Attack 4 could also produce varying reduction if different periods have different reduction mechanisms, but the definition says "all measurements in that period are reduced." It doesn't specify how they are reduced, could be constant subtraction or scaling. Typically, attack 4 is like a partial bypass where only a portion of the day is stolen. So there should be a clear period of normal consumption. We don't see any such period. So likely not.

Thus, Attack 3 is most probable.

Now, also consider Attack IEEE: on-off with zeros, not applicable.

So I'll classify as Manipulated, Attack 3.

Confidence: Medium? Because without knowing the exact original for that day, we can't be sure it wasn't a constant scaling with a naturally lower baseline. But the presence of unusually low min (4.4) when all reference week days have min >=10.35 strongly suggests manipulation beyond normal variability. So high confidence.

Reasoning: The query day shows a similar consumption trend (morning and evening peaks) as the reference week, but with significantly reduced magnitude. The mean (14.94) is much lower than any reference day mean (18.93-24.36), and the minimum (4.40) is far below the reference week minima (~10-12). The standard deviation is within range, suggesting the shape is preserved but scaled. A constant scaling factor would proportionally reduce min, but a factor fit to the mean (~0.6) would give min around 7, still higher than 4.4, indicating the scaling factor varies across samples—thus Attack 3. Attack 1 (constant scaling) was considered but ruled out due to inconsistent scaling ratios across the day (e.g., low min implies a different factor than the mean). Attack 2 was ruled out because there are no zero values. Attack 4 (theft over a period) was ruled out because no portion of the day appears unreduced. Attack 6 (cut-off cap) would show a flat ceiling, not observed.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated: The actual consumption of the meter is multiplied by different random factors (between 0 and 1) for each 15-minute sample, leading to a reduction that varies per reading. This mimics varying degrees of bypass or interference over time.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Both magnitude and trend are changed. The overall consumption is reduced, and while the general pattern (peaks and valleys) is still recognizable, the relative heights of peaks may be altered, and some values become abnormally low (e.g., the 4.40 reading).
- Possible technical causes: Can be caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, or false data injection where the attacker aims to lower the recorded consumption but perhaps in a less predictable way to avoid detection.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.
